Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of amino acids to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring can provide additional stabilization through π-π interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring can act as a ligand, enhancing the coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. Hydroxyl groups can form hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing sulfur groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can reduce the electron density available for coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group can act as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

**End of Rules**

Traditional Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.6 is significantly lower than the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules are not as effective in predicting the loading of modifiers on catalysts.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the model is not capturing the complexity of the data. The rules may not be comprehensive enough to account for the variability in the dataset.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values are likely problematic. For instance, Rule 1 and Rule 4 often have negative SHAP values in such cases, suggesting they may need refinement.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values should be scrutinized. Rule 1 and Rule 4 have shown high positive SHAP values in some cases, indicating potential overestimation.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The significant drop in accuracy from the reference metrics indicates that the current rules may not be capturing the necessary features or interactions effectively.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 1 and Rule 4 frequently contribute to discrepancies between predicted and experimental values. This suggests that these rules may need to be refined to better capture the nuances of the data.
   - Rule 7, which consistently shows a positive SHAP value, might be contributing to overestimation in some cases and should be reviewed for potential adjustments.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1**: Consider revising the conditions under which amino acid structures are considered beneficial. Explore additional structural features or interactions that might influence the loading.
   - **Rule 4**: Re-evaluate the impact of multiple hydroxyl groups. Investigate whether specific configurations or additional functional groups might alter their effect.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1 and Rule 4**: For cases where these rules contribute to underestimation, consider incorporating additional features or interactions that might enhance their predictive power.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce new rules or modify existing ones to capture a broader range of molecular features. This could involve integrating more complex interactions or considering additional functional groups.
   - Consider using ensemble methods or hybrid models that combine rule-based approaches with machine learning techniques to improve generalization.

4. **Iterative Refinement Using SHAP Insights:**
   - Use SHAP analysis to identify patterns or features that are consistently misrepresented by the current rules. Iteratively refine the rules to better align with these insights.
   - Conduct further experiments to validate the impact of proposed rule changes and assess their effect on model performance.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve both the accuracy and generalization of the model, ultimately leading to better predictions of modifier loading on catalysts.

    Target Name: yield

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of amino acids to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring can provide additional stabilization through π-π interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring can act as a ligand, enhancing the coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. Hydroxyl groups can form hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing sulfur groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can reduce the electron density available for coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group can act as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

This is likely due to the ability of amino acids to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring can provide additional stabilization through π-π interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring can act as a ligand, enhancing the coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. Hydroxyl groups can form hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can reduce the electron density available for coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a combination of carboxylic acid and hydroxyl groups (e.g., 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The presence of both groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with amino groups in conjunction with aromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The amino group can enhance electron donation to the aromatic system, stabilizing the catalyst.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with acetamido groups (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group may introduce steric hindrance or reduce effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with amino groups in conjunction with aromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The amino group can enhance electron donation to the aromatic system, stabilizing the catalyst.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with acetamido groups (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group may introduce steric hindrance or reduce effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups can decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures can provide additional π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of amino acids to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring can provide additional stabilization through π-π interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring can act as a ligand, enhancing the coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. Hydroxyl groups can form hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing sulfur groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with formyl groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group can act as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a combination of carboxylic acid and hydroxyl groups (e.g., 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The presence of both groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with amino groups in conjunction with aromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The amino group can enhance electron donation to the aromatic system, stabilizing the catalyst.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with acetamido groups (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group may introduce steric hindrance or reduce effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups can decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures can provide additional π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a combination of carboxylic acid and hydroxyl groups (e.g., 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The presence of both groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with amino groups in conjunction with aromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The amino group can enhance electron donation to the aromatic system, stabilizing the catalyst.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with acetamido groups (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group may introduce steric hindrance or reduce effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups can decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures can provide additional π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

Traditional Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.6571. However, this is a decrease from the previous validation accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be as effective as previous iterations.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The decrease in validation accuracy, along with the varied SHAP values, suggests potential overfitting. The model might be too tailored to the training data, failing to generalize well to the validation set. The consistent high SHAP values for certain rules across different molecules indicate that these rules might be too specific.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values (e.g., Rule 6 and Rule 13) might be problematic. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values (e.g., Rule 6 and Rule 13) might need adjustment.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The decrease in validation accuracy indicates that the current rules might not be capturing the underlying patterns as effectively as before.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that certain rules, such as Rule 6 and Rule 13, have a significant impact on the predictions. These rules might be contributing to overestimated or underestimated loadings, suggesting a need for refinement.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - Rules like Rule 6 (formyl groups) and Rule 13 (electron-withdrawing groups) have high positive SHAP values for overestimated loadings. Consider revising these rules to better capture the conditions under which these groups lead to high or low loadings.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - For underestimated loadings, rules with high negative SHAP values, such as Rule 6 and Rule 13, should be adjusted. This could involve reevaluating the conditions under which these groups are considered to lead to low yield.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential overfitting, consider simplifying some of the rules or combining similar rules to reduce complexity. This could help improve the model's ability to generalize to new data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules that consistently show high SHAP values across different molecules, as these are likely to have a significant impact on predictions.

5. **Incorporate Additional Features:**
   - Consider incorporating additional features or modifying existing ones to capture more nuanced interactions between modifiers and catalysts. This could involve exploring new chemical properties or structural features that might influence loading.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve both the accuracy and generalization of the model, leading to better predictions of modifier loading on catalysts.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring can act as a ligand, enhancing the coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. Hydroxyl groups can form hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing sulfur groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with formyl groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group can act as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a combination of carboxylic acid and hydroxyl groups (e.g., 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The presence of both groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with amino groups in conjunction with aromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The amino group can enhance electron donation to the aromatic system, stabilizing the catalyst.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with acetamido groups (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group may introduce steric hindrance or reduce effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups can decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures can provide additional π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of amino acids to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring can provide additional stabilization through π-π interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring can act as a ligand, enhancing the coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. Hydroxyl groups can form hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing sulfur groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with formyl groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group can act as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a combination of carboxylic acid and hydroxyl groups (e.g., 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The presence of both groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with amino groups in conjunction with aromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The amino group can enhance electron donation to the aromatic system, stabilizing the catalyst.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with acetamido groups (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group may introduce steric hindrance or reduce effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups can decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures can provide additional π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

Overall, the current rules show promising performance with high confidence and lift, indicating strong and meaningful associations. By focusing on increasing support and validating the rules, further improvements can be achieved.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Consider expanding the feature set to include more complex interactions or additional chemical properties that may influence loading. This could help address potential underfitting by providing the model with more information to learn from.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of amino acids to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring can provide additional stabilization through π-π interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring can act as a ligand, enhancing the coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. Hydroxyl groups can form hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing sulfur groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid, 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with formyl groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group can act as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with nitro groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, potentially enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains may cause steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups can donate electron density to the aromatic ring, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a combination of carboxylic acid and hydroxyl groups (e.g., 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The presence of both groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with amino groups in conjunction with aromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The amino group can enhance electron donation to the aromatic system, stabilizing the catalyst.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with acetamido groups (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group may introduce steric hindrance or reduce effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups can decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures can provide additional π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.6571. However, this is a decrease from previous iterations where the accuracy reached up to 0.6857. This suggests that while the rules are capturing some patterns, there is room for improvement.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The fluctuation in validation accuracy across iterations suggests potential overfitting. The model may be capturing noise in the training data rather than generalizable patterns. The consistent validation accuracy around 0.6-0.68 indicates that the model might not be learning effectively from the data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values (e.g., Rule 1 and Rule 6) might be overestimating the effect of certain features.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values (e.g., Rule 2 and Rule 3) might be underestimating the effect of certain features.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is potential for improvement. The model has not yet reached a local minimum, as indicated by the fluctuating accuracy scores. This implies that further refinement of the rules could lead to better performance.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that certain rules (e.g., Rule 1 and Rule 6) have a significant impact on the predictions. These rules should be closely examined and refined to ensure they are not contributing to overestimation or underestimation of the loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 1**: Consider revising the conditions under which amino acid structures with carboxylic acid and hydroxyl groups are considered beneficial. The SHAP values suggest that this rule might be too broad and could be leading to overestimation.
   - **Rule 6**: Re-evaluate the impact of formyl groups on aromatic rings. The current rule might be overemphasizing their negative impact, leading to overestimation in some cases.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 2**: Reassess the conditions for phenyl groups with carboxylic acid and hydroxyl or amino substituents. The rule might be too restrictive, leading to underestimation of their positive impact.
   - **Rule 3**: Consider expanding the scope of pyridine rings with electron-donating groups. The current rule might not fully capture their potential to enhance yield.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more diverse training data to help the model generalize better. This could involve incorporating more examples of edge cases where the current rules fail.
   - Regularize the model to prevent overfitting by using techniques such as dropout or L2 regularization.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an additional hydroxyl group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups enhances coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and a hydroxyl or amino substituent on the phenyl ring (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. These substituents enhance π-π interactions and electron donation.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional electron-donating groups (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The pyridine nitrogen and electron-donating groups enhance coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups on a short carbon chain (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl groups form hydrogen bonds, stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing sulfur groups without additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with formyl groups on aromatic rings without additional electron-donating groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group acts as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with nitro groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons and without compensating functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains cause steric hindrance, reducing catalyst efficiency.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings and additional electron-donating groups (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups donate electron density, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures and additional electron-donating groups (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures provide π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with acetamido groups and additional steric hindrance (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group introduces steric hindrance, reducing effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl without additional stabilizing interactions (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains without additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an additional hydroxyl group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups enhances coordination and stabilization of the catalyst.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and a hydroxyl or amino substituent on the phenyl ring (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) generally lead to high yield. These substituents enhance π-π interactions and electron donation.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional electron-donating groups (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The pyridine nitrogen and electron-donating groups enhance coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups on a short carbon chain (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl groups form hydrogen bonds, stabilizing the catalyst structure.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with sulfur groups without additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. Sulfur can lead to deactivation of the catalyst through strong binding or poisoning.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with formyl groups on aromatic rings without additional electron-donating groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The formyl group acts as an electron-withdrawing group, destabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with nitro groups on aromatic rings (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The nitro group can participate in electron transfer processes, enhancing the catalytic cycle.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with alkyl chains longer than six carbons and without compensating functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. Longer alkyl chains cause steric hindrance, reducing catalyst efficiency.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with methoxy groups on aromatic rings and additional electron-donating groups (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Methoxy groups donate electron density, stabilizing the catalyst complex.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with benzofuran or similar heterocyclic structures and additional electron-donating groups (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These structures provide π-π interactions and electronic stabilization.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with acetamido groups and additional steric hindrance (e.g., (2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The acetamido group introduces steric hindrance, reducing effective coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl without additional stabilizing interactions (e.g., 2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl) tend to result in low yield. These groups decrease electron density, reducing coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with hydroxyl groups on long alkyl chains without additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization when distanced by a long alkyl chain.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a combination of a carboxylic acid group and a pyridine ring without additional electron-donating groups (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The lack of additional electron-donating groups limits effective coordination.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring containing both electron-withdrawing and electron-donating groups (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The balance of these groups can stabilize the catalyst through resonance and inductive effects.
